Bonefish &amp; Tarpon Trust Issues Public Comment on NOAA Notice

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]23 Dec 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/bonefish-amp-tarpon-trust-issues-public-comment/docview/2472125144/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Bonefish+%26amp%3Bamp%3B+Tarpon+Trust+Issues+Public+Comment+on+NOAA+Notice&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-12-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Targeted News Service
WASHINGTON, Dec. 23 -- Aaron Adams, director of science and conservation at Bonefish and Tarpon Trust, West Palm Beach, Florida, has issued a public comment on the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration notice entitled "Aquaculture Opportunity Areas". The comment was posted on Dec. 21, 2020:
* * *
This comment lists some of the problems with siting an Aquaculture Opportunity Area in the eastern Gulf of Mexico.
The area being considered is too important for existing economically important fisheries, and presents too many existing environmental risks to allow aquaculture.
The recreational fisheries for bonefish, tarpon, and permit are economically important. For example, the flats fishery has an annual economic impact that exceeds $465 million in the Florida Keys, and $55 million in Belize. These species are a major component of the recreational fishery in the Everglades region with an annual economic impact exceeding $1 billion. The annual economic impact of the tarpon fishery in Charlotte Harbor, FL exceeds $120 million. More broadly, the annual economic impact of saltwater recreational fishing in Florida exceeds $12 billion. These fisheries depend upon healthy habitats, high water quality, and disease- and contaminant-free waters.
The establishment of offshore finfish aquaculture in the Gulf of Mexico insufficiently protects our recreational fisheries and their habitats. Our top concerns include:
- Fish farms create a high amount of waste, adding nutrients to the waters in which they are placed. These additional nutrients contribute to algae blooms and environmental degradation. Although the standard approach is to place offshore fish cages in areas with moderate currents so that the wastewater is carried away from the cage, the dilution of this waste does not remove it from the system. Since Florida's Gulf coast already suffers from red tides that are exacerbated by human-introduced nutrients, we are concerned that offshore fish cages will contribute to the issue of excessive nutrients. This is especially true given that red tide blooms start in an offshore environment that is normally nutrient-limited. Red tides cause large-scale fish kills and close shellfish harvest. Increasing red tide occurrence will negatively impact tarpon and the tarpon fishery, as well as other fisheries.
- The entire region is impacted by red tides. What happens when a red tide occurs and kills fish in offshore fish cages? Such a high density of dead fish is an environmental threat.
- The presence of large fish cages in offshore waters will aggregate wild fish, acting as a Fish Attracting Device (FAD), which may impact fish migrations and reproduction in two ways.
- First, the cages will likely attract sharks. Since tarpon seasonally migrate through areas preferred by offshore aquaculture, tarpon may be exposed to high encounter rates with sharks along their migration pathways. There are similar concerns for other migratory species.
- Second, for species that are already overfished, FADs, such as a fish cage, will increase the catch rate, and thus harvest, contributing to overfishing.
- Current evaluative criteria do not account for fish migratory pathways. Disruption of migrations can have population-level impacts on economically important wild fish species.
- Current evaluative criteria do not include acknowledgment of data-limited situations. Data are limited on many species that live in the Gulf of Mexico, including data identifying the types, spatial extent, and quality of essential habitats for many fish species. Thus, models being used to evaluate potential sites for aquaculture operations are fundamentally deficient.
- It would be beneficial to more proactively control the use of pharmaceuticals and other prophylactics. Since pharmaceutical contamination has been shown to affect fish behavior, migrations, and reproduction, this is a concern for native species. Indeed, preliminary data from BTT research shows that bonefish in the Florida Keys have numerous pharmaceuticals in their tissues. Adding more pharmaceuticals to the ecosystem via fish cages is not wise.
- More extensive pre-site selection work should be done to better describe the fisheries in locations proposed for offshore cages. At present, the assessment process does not include sufficient information to determine the existing fisheries and how they might be impacted. For areas that may impact existing fisheries, a system whereby aquaculture companies post a bond to cover negative impacts to existing fisheries should be considered. Similarly, a more rigorous monitoring system - before site selection as well as during cage operation - should be considered. This should include biological data (e.g., plankton community, fish community) and extensive water quality data (e.g., nutrients) over time to account for seasonal or other time-based changes.
- There is a high likelihood that offshore cages will not be safe from hurricanes. This is for two reasons. First, the Gulf of Mexico is a high-frequency hurricane region, so it is a near certainty that offshore cages will be in the path of a tropical storm or hurricane, as shown by consultation of the US National Hurricane Center database (https://coast.noaa.gov/hurricanes/). For example, since 1852, there have been 77 tropical storms or hurricanes that have passed within 65 miles of a site permitted to Kampachi Farms. Second, the Gulf of Mexico is so shallow that the standard approach to protect cages from storms - lowering the cages toward the bottom with an anchoring system - will not work. The Ekman Depth (depth to which wind-induced currents are felt) for Hurricane Florence (a Category 4 hurricane in 2018) was 250', much deeper than the depths (70') at which cage sites are being proposed. Impacts from hurricanes would include destruction of the cage, the cage breaking loose, escape of caged fish into the native population.
- The high density of fish that will be grown in cages will make these areas hotspots for disease and parasites. This has occurred in other areas where offshore aquaculture is conducted. We are concerned about the impacts of these diseases and parasites on tarpon and other native species. Similarly, escape of fish from cages may also contribute to spreading disease.
- Aquaculture activities that are currently suitable are those for oysters and clams in nearshore waters, which have proven to be sustainable with minimal environmental impact.
- Consideration should be given to land-based fish culture. Land-based operations allow the control of effluent and escapes, thus avoiding many of the concerns with open ocean cage culture.
Bonefish & Tarpon Trust is an international, science-based, 501(c)3 non-profit, conservation organization headquartered in Miami, FL. BTT is dedicated to the conservation of the economically and culturally important finfish species bonefish, tarpon, permit--the species, their habitats, and the larger fisheries they support. We work in a broad geography that spans the southeastern US, Gulf of Mexico, Caribbean Basin, and western North Atlantic.
* * *

Florida Agriculture &amp; Consumer Services Department Aquaculture Division Issues Public Comment on NOAA Notice

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]10 Dec 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/florida-agriculture-amp-consumer-services/docview/2468693531/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Florida+Agriculture+%26amp%3Bamp%3B+Consumer+Services+Department+Aquaculture+Division+Issues+Public+Comment+on+NOAA+Notice&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-12-10&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Targeted News Service
WASHINGTON, Dec. 10 -- Portia Sapp, director of the Aquaculture Division at the Florida Agriculture and Consumer Services Department, Tallahassee, has issued a public comment on the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration notice entitled "Aquaculture Opportunity Areas". The comment was written on Dec. 7, 2020, and posted on Dec. 9, 2020:
* * *
This letter serves as official comment from the Florida Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services in response to the NOAA "Aquaculture Opportunity Areas 2020" Request for Information (Docket # NOAA-NMFS-2020-0118). All comments below are written in the context of the Gulf of Mexico region; in particular, the Gulf coast of Florida. All references cited are listed after the written comments. We thank you for the opportunity to provide feedback on this effort and look forward to working with you in the future.
Sincerely,
Portia Sapp, Director
Division of Aquaculture
Florida Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services
* * *
1. Are there types of offshore aquaculture that these areas may or may not support, or are there other water depths and maximum distances from shore that should be considered, and why?
Offshore aquaculture at the proposed depths should focus on finfish production, or integrated multitrophic aquaculture. Seaweed and shellfish can both be produced at far shallower depths and not require the financial overhead of operating systems for those species that far offshore. However, the minimum acceptable depth will vary based on cage design and size, current speed, wave energy and hurricane risk as well as the specific species and life stage being raised.
Although there is no maximum distance from shore specified, siting AOAs must consider distances to and availability of necessary land-based infrastructure such as ports and marinas, hatcheries and nurseries, processing facilities and distribution networks. The time and expense spent on travel is a significant consideration for operations that should be included when siting AOAs. Locations too far offshore or too far from needed infrastructure may significantly reduce efficiency and viability of operations (viz-a-viz fuel, time, vessel maintenance, reduced health and quality of product, etc.).
In an expert survey conducted by the Florida Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services (FDACS) Division of Aquaculture in June 2019, this particular question was asked: "What do you consider an ideal distance to shore for an offshore aquaculture operation in Florida Gulf waters?" Answers varied from the specific (e.g., 5-9 nm, "a 30-minute boat ride") to the general (e.g., "Deep enough to raise fish and close enough to be practical") but made clear that there needs to be a balance between environmental considerations, competing uses of the space and practicality for business operations.
2. Are there specific locations or habitats within Federal waters of the Gulf of Mexico or Southern California that should be considered for AOAs? Are there specific locations that should be avoided? Please be as specific as possible and include latitude and longitude or defining landmarks. Please indicate why such areas should be considered or avoided, for example, favorable biological parameters, water quality (e.g., nutrients or other constituents that might make an area favorable), proximity to infrastructure (e.g., ports, processing plants, hatcheries or nurseries that could supply fingerlings for grow-out), relationship to other planned initiatives, etc.
Florida is ideally suited to lead the nation in aquaculture production in the coming decades. Florida aquaculture has more than doubled in farm abundance over the past 30 years and continues to expand in production volume and diversity. Florida's vast coastal region and climate make it ideal for aquaculture and the development of unique, high-value species such as cobia, snapper, grouper and jacks. In addition, Florida has a rich maritime culture and strong infrastructure in place to support expanding aquaculture. Florida has a large economic demand for fresh seafood, currently served at some 41,000 restaurants to 21.5 million residents and 126 million annual visitors. Florida has existing commercial and recreational fishing, aquaculture, and seafood jobs and business infrastructure, including seafood processors and wholesalers, distribution networks, markets and working waterfront communities throughout the state. Florida also has the bipartisan support of political leaders for developing offshore aquaculture in the state, including Commissioner of Agriculture Nikki Fried and Senator Marco Rubio.
In addition, the Florida Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services (FDACS) has also been working closely with the NOAA National Centers for Coastal Ocean Science (NCCOS) for over a year to identify specific regions in state waters that show potential for offshore aquaculture development.
We have conducted a scientifically-rigorous spatial siting analysis with over 250 GIS data layers, including: high resolution bathymetry and current speed; military; navigation; energy infrastructure; commercial shipping; commercial and recreational vessel traffic, including fishing; critical and essential fish habitat; HAPCs and other areas used for protection and management of natural resources; cultural resources; and recreation and tourism.
Based on this analysis of competing uses and site suitability, we have identified three potential regions in state waters that could support development of offshore aquaculture (Figure 1). Based on current and anticipated social license conflicts as well as the location of existing maritime and seafood infrastructure such as ports and processing facilities, the areas offshore of Pensacola and Apalachicola show the most promise for further exploration (see the green and blue areas in Figure 1 below). While federal and state waters will differ, siting AOAs in federal waters adjacent or near to areas that show high potential for aquaculture development in state waters could have multiple benefits (i.e., help facilitate more effective monitoring, enforcement and data collection efforts; streamline the federal review process when siting AOAs; create synergies within and among businesses in the seafood, aquaculture, tourism, restaurant and other maritime industries; and facilitate collaboration among the academic community and regulatory agencies).
The location of sensitive habitats, critical habitat and areas such as HAPCs and essential fish habitat should of course be considered when siting aquaculture operations. Federal waters along Florida's Gulf coast do contain hard bottom that is important for the life history of commercially important species, such as grouper and red snapper. Hard bottom as well as natural and artificial reefs should be avoided if there is potential for negative impacts with cultural and environmental resources. However, offshore structures are well known to act as aggregating devices for ecologically and economically-important fish species and other animals such as sharks and marine mammals (Bortone et al. 2011), thereby enhancing the effect of artificial reefs or natural hard bottom habitat for fishing and other ocean recreation. The potential for cooperation and connection between commercial fishing and offshore aquaculture should be considered when siting AOAs, as co-location may have positive synergistic effects and implications for commercial and recreational fishing, ocean recreation and fisheries management (see for example, Arechavala-Lopez et al. 2011, Callier et al. 2018, Clavelle et al. 2019).
Figure omitted: https://www.regulations.gov/contentStreamer?documentId=NOAA-NMFS-2020-0118-0023&attachmentNumber=1&contentType=pdf
4. Are there specific locations within Federal waters of the Gulf of Mexico or Southern California that should be avoided because of concerns about harmful algal blooms (HABs) or impaired water quality? Please specify whether these concerns are related to: (a) Aquaculture activities being impacted by HABs and impaired water quality, or (b) aquaculture activities contributing to HABs and impaired water quality?
Red tide (Karenia brevis) is a significant concern and has caused devasting economic and ecological impacts for coastal residents and businesses in southwest Florida for many years. Red tide most commonly occurs along the coastline between Collier and Hernando counties.
Hurricanes and currents occasionally entrench red tide into the Panhandle from the southwest, and the southern Atlantic coastline is also impacted occasionally. While other HAB species do occur in Florida waters, they are limited to nearshore environments and are not a concern for federal water aquaculture sites. At present there are no offshore, feed-added aquaculture facilities in any state or federal waters off of Florida's Gulf coast, meaning that these HABs are not induced by aquaculture activities. Although siting should consider HAB "hotspots", planning for HABs such as red tide should be included in any farm plan, similar to preparing for and mitigating impacts of storms and hurricanes.
5 The Florida state map archive for red tide is hosted by the Florida Fish and Wildlife Conservation Commission (FWC) online at: https://www.flickr.com/photos/myfwc/sets/72157635398013168/. Additional data and information for red tide in Florida can be found at FWC's website: https://myfwc.com/research/redtide/.
5. Is there ongoing environmental, economic, or social science research that would assist in the identification and implementation of AOAs in Federal waters of the Gulf of Mexico or Southern California? If so, please describe in as much detail as is available.
The social and cultural components of aquaculture in the Gulf of Mexico are perhaps the least well studied, as compared to environmental impacts, economics, technology, engineering, husbandry and animal health. Social license and public perception are consistently discussed as being crucial for aquaculture development (e.g., Knapp and Rubino 2016, Montgomery 2019) but to the best of our knowledge there is not a comprehensive literature on the topic specifically for the Gulf of Mexico (but see Camp et al. 2020). We know from current experience that public perception, misinformation and social license remain large hurdles to overcome in the Gulf of Mexico and the United States more broadly. Anecdotally and in practice we know that there is resistance to offshore aquaculture from vocal environmental groups and some commercial fishing groups in Florida. In the aforementioned FDACS 2019 expert aquaculture survey, "public perceptions or concerns" was ranked as one of the most negative interactions/impacts on farm operations and the permitting process (along with "access to capital or other financial considerations").
Notably, aquaculture presents a unique opportunity to diversify the business portfolio and income for existing fishers and others in the seafood industry, thus strengthening connections between the industries and enhancing resiliency of coastal communities overall. However, uncertainties remain about how and why fishers may participate, barriers to entry, the socioeconomic and sociocultural dynamics that may be a factor and what policy, management and institutional frameworks are needed for successful governance (e.g., Tango-Lowy and Robertson 2002, Pianka 2016, Morgan et al. 2017, Stoll et al. 2019). This work is ongoing globally and should be considered when siting AOAs.
At the same time, the FDACS Division of Aquaculture is currently planning initiatives to carry out such social science research for state waters of the Gulf of Mexico. Specifically, we plan to collaborate with stakeholders in the regions identified from the previously described NCCOS siting analysis (Pensacola and Apalachicola) to better understand their perceptions, values and sociocultural dynamics related to offshore aquaculture. Current commercial and recreational fishers, shellfish farmers and others in the seafood industry will be part of this work. Although we are planning to work with stakeholders on offshore aquaculture development in state waters, enhancing our understanding of stakeholder perceptions and values around offshore aquaculture should be applicable more broadly to AOAs in Florida.
6. Is there information that may not be readily available or accessible online that would be useful for AOA planning processes in Federal waters of the Gulf of Mexico and Southern California? This includes spatial data or geographic information system (GIS) layers representing environmental and socioeconomic considerations, or a point of contact for these data, for the following categories:
--Biophysical/oceanographic (wave climate, currents, bathymetry)
--Natural resources (minerals, energy resources, fishes and aquatic organisms, protected species and habitats, coral reefs, biodiversity)
--Social and cultural resources
--Government boundaries
--Industry (fishing, energy production, transportation, communication cables)
--Military
--Navigation
Spatial data for many of the mentioned layers are currently available online from the Florida Fish and Wildlife Conservation Commission (https://myfwc.com/research/gis/; Contact: Christi Santi; christi.santi@myfwc.com), Florida Department of Environmental Protection (https://floridadep.gov/otis/enterprise-application-services/gis; Contact: GIS.Librarian@dep.state.fl.us) and the Florida Department of State (Contact: Chris Fowler, christopher.fowler@dos.myflorida.com). Additional data gathered from a variety of state resources can be found at the State of Florida Geographic Data Portal online: https://geodata.myflorida.com/.
Data on social and cultural resources, military use, as well as fishing industry data are harder to come by and will likely require additional research. In particular, commercial fishing data as visualized and quantified using a combination of VMS and trip logbook data will be the best indicator of potential interactions with the commercial fishing industry in the Gulf (see for example, Cockrell 2018). These data are not publicly available and are currently held by NOAA's Southeast Regional Office and the NMFS Southeast Fisheries Science Center.
Data on land-based point source wastewater inputs (outfalls and plumes) relative to aquaculture sites are also important to consider and to our knowledge are not readily available. While wastewater inputs may not directly reach aquaculture sites in federal waters, indirect impacts such as nutrient transport on currents or triggering a harmful algal bloom that may reach an aquaculture site are important to consider. In populated coastal cities such as Tampa and Sarasota in southwest Florida, where frequent storms and aging infrastructure lead to unintentional and intentional releases of partially-treated wastewater, inducing red tide (Karenia brevis) blooms that may impact aquaculture operations are a real concern.
12. If states express interest in developing offshore aquaculture, should we also consider state waters as areas for future AOAs?
Without question, state waters should absolutely be considered for future development of AOAs. There is a steadily growing interest in the offshore aquaculture sector from both current and potentially new producers and entrepreneurs. This interest is not only for food production, but also for markets like biofuels, bioplastics, bait fish, ornamentals and restorative and regenerative aquaculture for environmental remediation and climate change mitigation. These production systems do not require the same water depths as the large food fish pens being focused on for federal water AOAs nor do they pose the same siting and management complexities. While our experience and comments are specific to Florida, we believe that the opportunities presented by developing offshore aquaculture apply for states throughout the Gulf of Mexico.
In Florida, there is a wealth of regulatory and policy experience among various state agencies as well as vast coastal infrastructure, a world-renowned research, technology and innovation community and generations of collective experience working in the seafood, aquaculture, agriculture and maritime industries. As noted above, the State of Florida has already been working with NCCOS on a spatial siting analysis for offshore aquaculture in state waters and is preparing to begin complementary social science research with coastal stakeholders.
Siting AOAs in state waters would present a tremendous opportunity for Florida to enhance its workforce, drive technology and innovation and strengthen coastal economies. Notably, some of the most economically-depressed communities in Florida - those that have been disproportionately impacted by multiple recent disasters, including Hurricanes Michael and Sally and the COVID-19 pandemic - are precisely the same communities that have traditionally driven their economies through coastal and ocean tourism, fishing, working waterfronts and other maritime businesses. Developing offshore aquaculture does not need to be at the expense of existing fishers and fishing businesses; on the contrary, aquaculture presents an opportunity to diversify income and thus make businesses and coastal economies stronger and more resilient. Providing federal resources through the creation and support of AOAs in Florida state waters will help provide new economic opportunity in these communities.
* * *

High-tech fish farming: Orangeburg County lands cutting-edge aquaculture industry

Author: Zaleski, Gene

Publication info: TCA Regional News ; Chicago [Chicago]29 Nov 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/wire-feeds/high-tech-fish-farming-orangeburg-county-lands/docview/2464941734/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anorthcentralnews&amp;atitle=High-tech+fish+farming%3A+Orangeburg+County+lands+cutting-edge+aquaculture+industry&amp;title=TCA+Regional+News&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-11-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Zaleski%2C+Gene&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=TCA+Regional+News&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Nov. 29With the consumption needs of an increasing world population putting greater stresses on environmental sustainability, there is a challenge of how to balance demand with supply.
Fishing experts project global aquaculture production will need to more than double in the next 40 years to feed the close to 10 billion people by the year 2050.
These same experts say there are many threats to fishing from the ocean, including overfishing that has reduced fish populations in some cases to the point of extinction. Industrialization has led to heavy metals pollution of the oceans. Plastic breakdown has infested marine life with micro-plastics.
The challenging scenario is one Pure Blue Fish U.S. founder and Chief Executive Officer Nimrod Litvak is addressing head-on and a key focus on the solution will be based in Orangeburg County.
Creating
an (aqua) culture
Litvak founded Pure Blue Fish, a fish farming company, about four years ago in Tel Aviv, Israel, to grow yellowtail fish.
The raising and eventual harvesting of the fish include recirculating aquaculture systems with zero-water discharge technology. The technology reduces water costs and pollution.
Last month, Litvak announced the company will invest $28.1 million to open its flagship U.S. operation in Orangeburg County at the John W. Matthews Jr. Industrial Park. The company will create 82 new jobs.
"Unlike most intensive aquaculture systems, our technology treats the water with biological and microbiological subsystems that enable our system to function at top efficiency without discharging polluted water, or any water, to the environment," Litvak said.
The Blue Fish system Pure Blue's Zero-Water Discharge Recirculating Aquaculture Systems (RAS) Technology was developed by Professor Jaap van Rijn of the Hebrew University of Jerusalem in Israel.
RAS is a touted as a new way to farm fish in a safe, productive and sustainable way.
Instead of the traditional method of growing fish outdoors, this system raises fish at high densities in a "controlled" environment typically indoors.
The technology allows farmers to control environmental conditions year-round.
The technology removes the fish waste through converting ammonia to nitrate, converting nitrate to nitrogen gas, which evaporates; and converting organic-rich sludge and fish waste to a gaseous phase.
The processes provide a healthy environment for freshwater and saltwater aquaculture and minimize the discharge of waste.
The zero-water discharge RAS also treats the water circulating in the system by biotech and micro biotech technologies, according to the company.
This helps to save high costs of water and the cost of treating water going in; prevents polluting water from going out to the environment, and allows fish aquaculture to prosper on land away from oceans and lakes, Litvak said.
Pure Blue touts the RAS system as:
 Being suitable for a wide range of fish species.
 Providing optimal growing conditions to speed up production.
 Providing a clean, no hormones, fresh and healthy fish.
 Helping reduce exposure to diseases, due to unique growing methods.
There are challenges that include high initial capital investment, the need to capture a market and the need to build a brand name.
The Orangeburg project
In Orangeburg, Blue Fish will build a facility that will be 50,000 to 100,000 square feet at the John Matthews Industrial Park on about 30 acres.
The interior of the building is currently in the design phase. It is expected to be completed by 2022. It will be privately funded and owned by the company.
The company will raise the yellowtail fish from fingerling to the nursery stage, where the fish will stay a period of two months and grow under close observation.
The fish then enter into the grow-out phase of about one year where they reach a size of about four pounds. The fish then go into the final stage of harvesting, sorting by size and packaging.
The fish will go fresh to the market in eight hours, according to Blue Fish.
There will be about 18,500 fingerlings in each production cycle and six productions a year, according to the company.
Pure Blue Fish's South Carolina operations will serve as a production and packaging facility for distribution to the restaurant market.
The plant will start with annual production capacity of 150-200 tons of fish a year and 11-16 employees. It will operate around the clock. The initial output is expected nine months after the plant is operational.
A packing/processing house will be built to serve the fish plant after the farm reaches 600 tons of annual production.
Eventually, the production facility plans to produce 3,000 tons of fish a year.
Yellowfish
Yellowtail is a species of sturdy ocean fish in the jack family.
The fish is typically harvested wild off the coast of central Japan, southern California and Baja, California, and farmed in Mexico and Australia, according to the company.
The fish is mostly eaten as sushi or sashimi in fine Japanese restaurants, or grilled and served widely in other cuisines. In retail shops and supermarkets, the whole fish is sold, as well as fillets.
Meeting demand
Litvak notes the demand for fish is there.
Citing researchers from Florida Atlantic University, Litvak says the United States was the fifth largest seafood consumer in the world in 2015 and ranked third in wild-caught volume.
FAU researchers also say the Unites States is the largest importer of seafood products in the world. In 2017 alone, about 5.9 billion pounds of seafood were consumed with more than 50% of seafood consumed coming from aquaculture.
Less than 1% is produced in the U.S.
Orangeburg County
seen as big catch
Litvak says the project is a win-win in that it provides the county a locally grown fish by a local workforce for local consumption. The county will also become a production and distribution center and will offer a healthy food option.
Litvak cites the Orangeburg area as a suitable location with the needed infrastructure and economical utility rates. The company also cited the available training for company employees and attractive manufacturing incentives.
The average wage at the Orangeburg plant is expected to be between $18 and $25 an hour. Orangeburg County's average manufacturing wage is $15 an hour.
Orangeburg lures
Blue Fish
The company is receiving traditional incentives such as fee-in-lieu of taxes and a state grant for road infrastructure improvements.
The Coordinating Council for Economic Development has also approved job-development credits related to the project.
The company looked throughout the Southeast from Virginia down to Florida, but chose Orangeburg due to its logistics, pro-business environment and technical employee skill sets.
Blue Fish is the 22nd international company to call Orangeburg County home.

____________________________________________________________

Environmental Defense Fund, Monterey Bay Aquarium Issue Public Comment on Corps of Engineers Proposed Rule

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]23 Nov 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/environmental-defense-fund-monterey-bay-aquarium/docview/2463280268/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Environmental+Defense+Fund%2C+Monterey+Bay+Aquarium+Issue+Public+Comment+on+Corps+of+Engineers+Proposed+Rule&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-11-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Targeted News Service
WASHINGTON, Nov. 23 -- Eric Schwaab, senior vice president for oceans and ecosystems at the Environmental Defense Fund, and Margaret Spring, chief conservation and science officer at the Monterey Bay Aquarium, California, has issued a public comment on the Corps of Engineers proposed rule entitled "Proposal To Reissue and Modify Nationwide Permits". The comment was written on Nov. 16, 2020, and posted on Nov. 17, 2020:
* * *
On behalf of the millions of members and supporters of Environmental Defense Fund (EDF) and the Monterey Bay Aquarium, we write to comment on three proposed nationwide permits (NWP): the proposed NWP B for Finfish Mariculture Activities, the proposed renewal of NWP 48 for Commercial Shellfish Mariculture Activities, and proposed NWP A for Seaweed Mariculture Activities. While we support sustainable aquaculture in U.S. waters, NWP B is not appropriate to cover such a broad range of finfish activities that, in many cases, pose significant risks of environmental impacts. Given this diversity of activities and potential impacts, we are unable to propose appropriate limitations for NWP B. We urge the Administration to withdraw and reconsider NWP B and include additional opportunities for input from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) in siting potential offshore aquaculture facilities. Nationwide permits may be suitable to regulate shellfish and kelp aquaculture if the production systems, impacts and best practices are well known and enforced. However, as proposed, NWP 48 and NWP A lack sufficient scientific justification and conditions for protections related to eelgrass, native species and cumulative impacts. These proposed nationwide permits should be revised with additional scientific justification, appropriate conditions and best practices and recirculated for public comment.
In significant respects, the challenge of permitting finfish aquaculture activities in the U.S. exclusive economic zone (EEZ) stems from the lack of a clear governance structure for this emerging use. NWP B does not have legal standards to prevent the full suite of potential environmental impacts, including escapes, siting, feed, sustainability, and other issues specific to finfish aquaculture. We believe that working to establish consensus around the appropriate standards, including pursuing more robust siting and additional research to answer open questions, would be a more durable approach that could meet the dual objectives of finfish aquaculture development and environmental protection. Further, reliance upon public comments to propose limitations on the three aquaculture NWPs is not an appropriate strategy to alleviate the current regulatory uncertainty and lack of financing that has significantly limited U.S. aquaculture development. Ultimately, U.S. offshore aquaculture requires a new comprehensive legal framework based on science, transparency and stakeholder engagement.
As a legal matter, nationwide permits are intended for activities similar in nature that cause minimal environmental effects./1
As described in further detail below, NWP B could permit activities of very different types in vastly different areas with potentially significant environmental impacts. And given the wide range of activities that could be authorized and the emerging state of the science and management of offshore finfish aquaculture, we are unable to provide meaningful terms and conditions that might address their environmental impacts. Absent a comprehensive framework, permitting finfish aquaculture undertaken on an individual, project specific basis that takes into account details about siting, structure, species, operations, and environmental conditions is necessary to ensure that aquaculture facilities do not pose unacceptable risks to safety and the environment. NWP A and NWP 48 can be addressed through providing more specific conditions and best practices in the proposed NWPs to address potential impacts on marine resources.
I. Finfish Mariculture Projects Are Not an Appropriate Subject of NWPs
NWPs are appropriate where the activities authorized "are similar in nature, will cause only minimal adverse environmental effects when performed separately, and will have only minimal cumulative adverse effect on the environment."/2
These parameters are critical, given that NWPs eliminate significant public participation through avoiding project-specific public notice requirements, NEPA compliance, and adoption of mitigation measures. They are intended to be used in cases where the Corps determines that the NWP itself provides sufficient information regarding the regulated activity for the public to understand the extent of the regulated activity and the associated environmental effects. Proposed NWP B fails to meet these standards.
A. All finfish mariculture activities are not similar in nature
As the Corps recognizes in its proposed rule, "[t]he impacts of mariculture activities on the environment are strongly influenced by how they are operated, including which species are being produced, stocking density, how the fish are being fed, and location."/3
Finfish mariculture activities are not similar in nature, particularly with respect to offshore farms. Offshore finfish farms are more exposed to wind and waves, and therefore usually require advanced technology to operate./4
While the challenges of anchoring and operating at sea are common to all offshore and coastal mariculture projects, the solutions to these challenges may be very different.
Offshore aquaculture projects may require any of the following: (i) heavy-duty moorings in deep sea water; (ii) offshore systems for the containment of aquatic crops; (iii) sea-going work boats fully equipped with cranes and crop harvesting and handling equipment; (iv) offshore feed storage and feed distribution systems; (v) automatic or partly automated feeding systems; (vi) mechanization of husbandry and maintenance tasks; (vii) remote monitoring and control systems; and (viii) increased production size to generate economies of scale./5
A wide range of designs and concepts have been developed for finfish mariculture to allow the practice to adapt to challenging ocean conditions. A number of these design concepts evolved out of offshore oil and gas rig designs./6
These include bottom-supported platforms, jack-up rigs and monopods, floating and semi-submersible platforms, including conventional ships and barges, as well as net pens supported between moored spar buoys./7
Frequently, offshore fish cages will be enclosed and completely submerged to avoid the wave stress placed on floating structures./8
Currently, a variety of submersible and semi-submersible sea cages exist on the market./9
This diversity is not limited to the types of structures employed, but also the species proposed to be cultivated. The draft NWP lists at least nine species that could be cultivated/10 and there is no limitation or restriction on what or where species can be cultivated, leaving open the option that finfish species that are not native to an area could be introduced. The draft decision document acknowledges this variability as well, noting that "alterations will vary, depending on the specific characteristics of the specific activity authorized by this NWP and the environmental setting in which the NWP activity may occur. The environmental setting will vary from site to site, and from region to region across the country."/11
The variety of designs, systems, and species available for finfish mariculture, combined with the need to adapt each system to unique regional and local environmental conditions, guarantees that each project will be substantially different than the next. The variety of finfish mariculture projects precludes these installations from being authorized under a NWP.
B. Finfish mariculture activities are likely to have greater than minimal adverse environmental impacts
The proposed rule does not provide sufficient information and analysis to conclude that all finfish aquaculture projects authorized under NWP B would result in minimal adverse environmental effects. If "the effect of a general permit will be more than minimal, either individually or cumulatively, the Corps cannot issue the permit."/12
Given that the proposed NWP would authorize very large facilities holding various types of species throughout the largest EEZ in the world,/13 it cannot meet this requirement.
Finfish mariculture projects, like certain commercial fishing gear, can present a serious risk of entangling or injuring marine mammals and other wildlife. At particular risk are large whales, including the endangered North Atlantic right whale/14 and Humpback whale./15
The risk presented to marine mammals cannot be properly evaluated or mitigated when the NWP would authorize finfish mariculture projects that could take place anywhere in an extremely large area and could have any number of operational components. The draft decision document acknowledges this potential issue, but takes no steps to avoid or mitigate potential impacts to marine mammals or other wildlife./16
Likewise, finfish aquaculture farms authorized under the NWP could result in significant cumulative impacts on marine wildlife and the environment. The failure of aquaculture equipment and gear can result in the release of cultivated, potentially non-native fish, which risks significant impacts through cross-breeding with native species, genetic mutation, disease proliferation, and new predation patterns./17
These impacts cannot be properly assessed and mitigated because the Corps simply lacks the information necessary to make an informed decision on those impacts on a nationwide basis./18
While the draft NWP acknowledges that "[s]patial planning for mariculture activities in federal waters . . . can be an important tool for siting these facilities to manage impacts on the aquatic environment,"/19 the NWP does not provide for such planning and would eliminate project-specific public notice and comment that would facilitate responsible site selection.
The proposed rule asserts that these issues can be addressed for individual applications through review by district engineers and the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency pursuant to a National Pollution Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permit./20
However, in Coalition to Protect Puget Sound Habitat v. U.S. Army Corps of Engineers,/21 the court invalidated an NWP that relied on similar reasoning:
The problems here are that the Corps' minimal impact determinations were entirely conclusory and the regional conditions that it assumed would minimize impacts were not in place at the time NWP 48 was adopted. The record is devoid of any indication that the Corps considered regional data, catalogued the species in and characteristics of the aquatic environments in which commercial shellfish aquaculture activities occur, considered the myriad techniques, equipment, and materials used in shellfish aquaculture, attempted to quantify the impacts the permitted activity would likely have on the identified species and characteristics, or evaluated the impacts of the as-yet-unknown regional conditions. Faced with incredible diversity in both the environment and the activities permitted under NWP 48, the Corps effectively threw up its hands and turned the impact analyses over to the district engineers . . . This abdication of responsibility is not authorized under the CWA or NEPA./22
Pursuant to this decision, the Corps has the obligation to analyze and quantify the adverse impacts of its proposed permit and cannot defer that analysis to individual district engineers or another agency./23
C. The Decision Document does not satisfy NEPA
The Decision Document relies on an environmental assessment (EA) to meet NEPA requirements, but the proposed action of allowing for widespread aquaculture may significantly affect the quality of the human environment,/24 necessitating an environmental impact statement (EIS).
Indeed, the draft decision document appears to acknowledge that an EIS is required, noting that there "may be unavoidable environmental risks associated with finfish mariculture activities,"/25 which triggers preparation of an EIS under NEPA. It further states that finfish mariculture activities "may cause physical, chemical, and biological changes to marine and estuarine waters,"/26 indicating that an analysis of alternatives including permit limitations, conditions, or mitigation measures to address these effects would be necessary as part of an EIS.
The Decision Document acknowledges:
Since there is considerable variation across the country in terms of the types of aquatic resources, the ecological functions and services they provide, and their resilience and resistances to disturbances caused by NWP activities, other human activities, and natural disturbances, the environmental consequences of the issuance of this NWP will vary by site and by region. Given the geographic scope in which this NWP can be used to authorize activities that require [Corps] authorization and the wide variability in aquatic resource structure, functions, and dynamics from site to site and from region to region, there are also considerable challenges in characterizing the potential environmental consequences of the issuance of this NWP at a national scale./27
Such variability makes a proper evaluation of environmental impacts impossible in an EA, which is limited to "briefly provid[ing] sufficient evidence and analysis for determining whether to prepare an environmental impact statement" and "briefly discuss[ing] the purpose and need for the proposed action, alternatives... and the environmental impacts of the proposed action and alternatives."/28
While the Decision Document attempts to address this problem by generally noting that structures and works in navigable waters may alter ecological functions, deferring site-specific analysis to district engineers was recently ruled to be insufficient./29
Similarly, the Corps' impact analysis disclaims any responsibility for environmental review of "activities that do not require [Corps] authorization, such as the addition or removal of finfish from the cages or net pens, feeding activities, the administration of antibiotics and other therapeutics to keep the cultivated finfish healthy, the use of chemicals to control sea lice and other parasites, and the use of antifouling agents."/30
But these impacts are the direct result of the permit being issued and must be examined./31
The Corps further notes that "[f]or the national-scale analysis of the potential environmental consequences presented in this section, it is not possible to quantify the relative contributions of the various activities."/32
However, "general statements about possible effects and some risk do not constitute a hard look absent a justification regarding why more definitive information could not be provided."/33
For example, as discussed above finfish escapement can be a significant risk of offshore aquaculture./34
While the Decision Document notes that serious issues can arise through improper installation and maintenance of the aquaculture gear approved by the Corps, it does not analyze the risks nor alternatives that would include robust inspections and enforcement as required by NEPA, instead appearing to rely upon the hope that "project proponents are responsible for designing and properly maintaining their finfish mariculture facilities, which should help reduce the potential for escapes."/35
While the Corps may not have the authority to regulate finfish escapes,/36 it can require that structures installed in navigable waters are properly designed and maintained to prevent them./37
An analysis of the identified environmental risks is necessary.
Similarly, the Decision Document notes that "Cultivating finfish species in ocean waters outside their native ecoregions should be considered a high risk activity that could potentially have substantial adverse ecological and socio-economic outcomes;"/38 finfish have low genetic diversity and interbreeding between wild and captive fish can reduce the ability of those species to survive; and describes additional environmental risks posed by finfish mariculture, but does not analyze the risks described./39
This level of general description, without an analysis of the potential effects or impacts, does not comply with NEPA./40
As noted above, rather than performing the detailed level of environmental review required by NEPA, the Corps proposes to have district engineers undertake the required environmental evaluation./41
While tiering review and decision-making is permissible under NEPA, there must first exist a national decision document that evaluates the impacts of the proposed activity in light of any regional conditions imposed to tier from./42
The Decision Document fails to undertake that analysis. Instead, cumulative analysis is limited to two NEPA assessments - one at national scale and one at a regional scale. It is unlikely that these NEPA assessments can capture the full suite of cumulative impacts without more specific species and operational information. The District Engineer has the authority to assess and approve permits based on these NEPA analyses in a decision document. It is unclear how the NWP will assess cumulative impacts without any standards in place.
The lack of analysis in the NWP is of particular concern because the NWP process itself has very little public engagement and transparency. The NWP is designed to minimize federal review (including NEPA) and fast-track permitting - the applicant is approved as long as they meet NWP regional requirements and pre-construction notifications, nearly all of which are within the discretion of the District Engineer.
II. The Permitted Activities Are Too Variable and Uncertain for the Public to Recommend Limitations
In this proposed rule, the Corps has requested that the public recommend limits to the scope of the proposed NWPs, such that use of the procedure would be appropriate./43
The Corps often solicits such information and then issues the final NWP with those qualifications it finds necessary./44
Unfortunately, here the scope of activities that could take place under all three NWPs is so broad that we cannot suggest such limitations at this time. For example, potential impacts could vary depending on the type of structure used and where they are located; what may be workable in the Gulf of Mexico might not be sufficient dozens of miles from the coast of Guam or Alaska. The scope of the NWPs is so broad that it is impossible to provide practical and appropriate parameters for siting, control of escapes, and other issues, without a much more specific and robust discussion of limiting conditions and standards in the proposed NWPs.
III. If the Corps Intends to Limit the NWPs, the APA Requires Reproposing the Action
Under the Administrative Procedure Act (APA), the public is entitled to notice and the opportunity to comment on information that is central to an agency's decision./45
This process provides for the "robust debate of competing and frequently complicated policy considerations having far-reaching implications,"/46 such as approving finfish aquaculture facilities for all waters of the United States.
The draft NWPs lack information necessary for informed public comment such as the types of structures that will be authorized, the species that may be cultivated, what limitations may be placed on finfish and kelp operations, as well as discussion of potential environmental effects or how any such effects may be mitigated. As such, they do not contain sufficient information to inform public comment./47
In the event that the Corps chooses to apply limiting conditions to the NWPs, we ask that the agency repropose them so that the public has the opportunity to comment on the revised proposed NWPs.
IV. NOAA's Role is Unclear
Under the recent Executive Order 13921, Promoting American Seafood Competitiveness and Economic Growth,/48 NOAA was designated the lead agency for aquaculture projects located outside of the waters of any State or Territory and within the EEZ, recognizing the important role that NOAA plays in managing marine resources./49
While the Corps currently consults with NOAA under the Endangered Species Act, Magnuson-Stevens Fishery Conservation and Management Act, and Marine Mammal Protection Act, this consultation is limited to specific considerations of impacts to endangered and threatened species, commercial fisheries, and marine mammals. While NOAA has a significant amount of expertise in these areas, it also can provide valuable guidance in site selection, and navigating potential conflicts between aquaculture projects, marine resources, other ocean users, and wild-capture fisheries. The proposed nationwide mariculture permits provide no role for NOAA in these efforts. The Corps would benefit from NOAA's scientific expertise on species selection, farming methods and other technical considerations necessary for evaluation of applicants or monitoring of operations.
NOAA is the appropriate agency to provide this expertise in reviewing and assigning specific permit terms.
V. Conclusion
We support responsible and environmentally sustainable aquaculture. Therefore, while we believe the NWP approach is inappropriate for finfish aquaculture, we stand ready to work with the Corps, NOAA, the industry, wild-capture fishermen, environmentalists, and others to help develop science-based regulatory standards that will allow for offshore aquaculture to add jobs and provide sustainable seafood to our nation while protecting the marine environment and existing uses. We also respectfully request that the Corps to incorporate specific conditions and best practices for NWP 48 and NWP A to protect marine resources and the environment, which should be recirculated for public review and comment.
Eric Schwaab, Senior Vice President, Oceans and Ecosystems, Environmental Defense Fund
Margaret Spring, Chief Conservation and Science Officer, Monterey Bay Aquarium
* * *

National Aquaculture Association Issues Public Comment on Corps of Engineers Proposed Rule

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]23 Nov 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/national-aquaculture-association-issues-public/docview/2463084776/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=National+Aquaculture+Association+Issues+Public+Comment+on+Corps+of+Engineers+Proposed+Rule&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-11-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Targeted News Service
WASHINGTON, Nov. 23 -- Jim Parsons, president of the National Aquaculture Association, Tallahassee, Florida, has issued a public comment on the Corps of Engineers proposed rule entitled "Proposal To Reissue and Modify Nationwide Permits". The comment was written and posted on Nov. 16, 2020:
* * *
The National Aquaculture Association1 offers for your consideration the following comments relative to the use of the term "mariculture," Nationwide Permit 48, Shellfish Mariculture Activities, Nationwide Permit A, Seaweed Mariculture Activities, and Nationwide Permit B, Marine Finfish Mariculture Activities. We request that the references provided within our comments be included in your Decision Document.
We recommend that each Corps District office add to their reference library:
Aquaculture Perspectives of Multi-Use Sites in the Open Ocean
Bela H. Buck and Richard Langan (editors)
Springer (open access publication)
(https://www.springer.com/gp/book/9783319511573 accessed November 6, 2020)
Aquaculture Production Systems
James H. Tidwell (editor)
World Aquaculture Society and Wiley-Blackwell
Environmental Best Management Practices for Aquaculture
Craig S. Tucker and John A. Hargreaves (editors)
US Aquaculture Society and Wiley-Blackwell
Shellfish Aquaculture and the Environment
Sandra E. Shumway (editor)
Wiley-Blackwell
Mariculture versus Aquaculture
We appreciate the effort by the Corps to distinguish the culture location through the use of the archaic term "mariculture." We respectfully suggest that the Corps follow the lead of the National Aquaculture Policy Act of 1980 and add modifying terms to "aquaculture" to create improved specificity. Examples could be "freshwater," "marine," "coastal marine," or "offshore." Relative to the NWPs, we suggest dropping the term "activity" since aquaculture is a farming practice and adopting the terms Marine Shellfish Aquaculture, Seaweed Aquaculture (seaweeds are marine plants; there is no need to be redundant) and Offshore Marine Finfish Aquaculture to define the activity and where it may occur.
Nationwide Permit 48, Shellfish Aquaculture
The East Coast Shellfish Growers Association and the Pacific Coast Shellfish Growers Association have thoroughly reviewed the proposed revisions to this nationwide permit. We support their request that primary literature should be cited to support your decision making for potential impacts related to production practices, production gear, submerged aquatic vegetation and mechanical harvest. We request that their references and analysis be accepted and incorporated into the final rule where appropriate. We also recommend that an agency information exchange occur with Pacific Shellfish Institute, NOAA Science Centers and state Sea Grant or Land Grant Programs such as Connecticut, Florida, Georgia, Mississippi-Alabama, Maine, New Hampshire, North Carolina, South Carolina, Virginia, and Washington that have researched and/or published papers describing potential environmental effects and how to mitigate those effects. A summary analysis of these efforts for the period 1970-2018 is posted here: http://masgc.org/assets/uploads/publications/1718/19-092.pdf.
The context in which shellfish are farmed in the United States is important when considering environmental effects. The 22 coastal states lease submerged marine lands for shellfish under the rubric of the public trust doctrine. Fundamentally, farms are required to maintain public resources held in trust by the states and return benefits for the use in the form of rent or farm-raised shellfish for human consumption (O'Connell 2018). We recommend recognizing this fundamental tenet of governance that while outside the Corps' authority is a significant societal action by each coastal state to protect the marine environment.
We believe the sentence in the proposed revision, "Commercial shellfish mariculture operations may use chemicals to control fouling organisms" is incorrect. Chemical use or the potential introduction of toxic chemicals is regulated by the Interstate Shellfish Sanitation Conference directly related to the waters in which shellfish are farmed and harvested, shellfish farming practices and beyond the farm gate in the human food supply chain by the Food and Drug Administration.
The Interstate Shellfish Sanitation Conference, a federal/state cooperative program recognized by the U. S. Food and Drug Administration, is focused on promoting and improving the sanitation of farmed and wild harvested shellfish (oysters, clams, mussels and scallops) moving in interstate commerce through federal/state cooperation and uniformity of State shellfish programs. All coastal states that farm or wild-harvest shellfish for human consumption are members and implement in state regulations the Guide for the Control of Molluscan Shellfish. Inherent to the Guide are provisions to prevent the introduction of toxic chemicals to shellfish intended for human consumption.
Beyond the farmgate, shellfish processors must implement Food and Drug Administration, Seafood HACCP. The program requires an analysis for potential hazards in shellfish for human consumption that includes Chapter 9, Environmental Chemical Contaminants and Pesticides (FDA 2020). Seafood processors, including shellfish, must assess and, in some cases test, the products that receive from the farmer or wild-harvest for identified chemicals and develop critical control points that will trigger controls to prevent hazardous chemicals from entering the human seafood market chain.
We recommend that the Corps recognize as normal farming practices the methods utilized to harvest shellfish through mechanical means (i.e., power rakes, hydraulic rakes, scrapes, suction harvesters, etc.) as provided in 33 CFR Sec. 323.2(d)(3)(iii) (Creswell and McNevin 2008). The function of these mechanical harvesters is to replace the use of hand tools and to separate farmed bivalve shellfish from benthic sediments. In all cases farmers do not wish to displace or remove sediment as this material will serve as the planting bed for future production. In essence the sediments are exempted "incidental fall back" materials. Only shellfish or their valves are removed 33 CFR Sec. 323.2(d)(1) (Stokesbury et al. 2011).
Nationwide Permit A, Seaweed Aquaculture
We support the creation of a nationwide permit for seaweed aquaculture. Seaweed farming has a relatively short US history and the foresight of the Corps to propose a nationwide permit that includes all of US marine waters is a welcomed development (Kim et al. 2019). Seaweed aquaculture as practiced in the United States does not result in the discharge of dredged and fill materials. Seaweed production is entirely passive. Young plants are "seeded" to anchored and suspended ropes for grow-out. Growing plants derive nutrients from the water column and farmers periodically visit farm sites to untangle lines and check mooring anchor number and condition (Flavin et al. 2013: Redmond et al. 2014; Freitag 2017). We disagree with the statement in the notice that "seaweed mariculture activities may alter estuarine and marine habitats utilized by endangered or threatened species." The production gear and passive growth of native marine plants improves water quality (Kim et al. 2014; Kim et al. 2017), provides ecosystem services (Froelich et al. 2019; Theuerkauf et al. 2019) and creates refuge and a potential source of food through the mobile and encrusting animals associated with the farms. We recommend that if District Engineers have questions about seaweed aquaculture to utilize a National Sea Grant Program Seaweed Hub as a source of information and expertise. In addition, we offer our assistance to organize farm tours for the US Army Corps of Engineers of active operations.
Nationwide Permit B, Marine Finfish Aquaculture
We support the creation of a nationwide permit for finfish aquaculture in coastal and oceanic waters and note that the Corps is currently in a permitting process for a Florida Sea Grant Program funded experimental and demonstration sea cage farm in the Gulf of Mexico.
We disagree with the statement in the notice that "finfish mariculture activities may alter estuarine and marine habitats utilized by endangered or threatened species." The federal permitting process for offshore farms requires interagency consultations, as authorized by the National Environmental Policy Act, to enforce the provisions of the Endangered Species Act, Marine Mammal Protection Act, Migratory Bird Treaty Act and Magnuson-Stevens Fishery Conservation and Management Act to prevent injury or death to listed species, marine mammals and birds and to prohibit unpermitted fishery harvest, possession or sale. Price and Morris (2013) evaluated entanglements across the globe and summarized:
"At modern fish farms, impacts to predatory sharks and marine mammals are being minimized with improved net technologies and removal of dead fish from cages to prevent predation on cultured fish. Siting away from known aggregation sites and installing rigid predator exclusion nets are effective at preventing negative impacts to cultured fish, farm structures and marine predators. Acoustic deterrent devices are not consistently useful against sea lions and seals and may have deleterious impacts to non-target marine mammals. In the U.S., nonlethal interventions to prevent marine mammal predation are preferred.2 At marine fish farms, entanglement in the farm structures may pose a slight threat to sea turtles, dolphins, whales and seabirds. Keeping lines taut and the water free of debris are effective at minimizing or eliminating conflict with marine mammals and turtles (page iv)."
Unlike fishing gear that is designed to intentionally "catch" animals, aquaculture gear is designed contain animals being cultured without hurting them or any wild animals that may occur around farms. A second in-depth analysis summarized interactions by at-risk sea turtles, sea birds, sharks and marine mammals with aquaculture production gear to inform the federal permitting process (Price et al. 2017). The goal of the assessment was to strengthen the ability of the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration and other regulatory agencies to make science-based decisions and recommendations as part of the review and consultation process required by the National Environmental Policy Act to permit nearshore and offshore aquaculture operations.
The authors found few at-risk species and aquaculture production gear interactions. As a surrogate they examined analogous fishery gear and then suggested preventative measures to include farm siting to avoid migration routes and resting, feeding and breeding habitats; farm worker training to avoid feeding or harassing visiting animals; properly tensioned lines; installation and maintenance of predation prevention nets; and similar commonsense practices (pages 57-58).
In addition, individuals and companies interested in siting a farm can avail themselves of publicly available farm siting tools to avoid conflict, or impact on, at-risk species and critical habitat. The NOAA National Centers for Coastal Ocean Science posts 83 aquaculture mapping and informational publications to the Coastal Aquaculture Planning Portal. The siting tools posted to the Portal can be state-specific (Alabama, Connecticut, Delaware, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts, New Hampshire, New Jersey, New York, North Carolina, Rhode Island, Texas, Washington), regional (Gulf of Mexico, Northwest Pacific, Southeast United States, mid-Atlantic) or habitat (essential fish habitat, marine protected areas, estuaries) oriented. Belle and Nash (2008) summarized the challenges associated with siting farms in the dynamic oceanic environment, noted environmental benefits and suggested site assessment criteria. The Belle and Nash reported:
"The health, welfare, and quality of the aquatic animals produced in net pens are directing impacted by the quality of water surrounding and flowing through pens. Importantly, producers using net pens have little ability to protect their animals from degraded water quality and other environmental influences. Thus, any effect that management practices have on water quality or the local environment also directly impacts culture animal performance and farm economic returns. The intimate linkage between environmental quality, cultured animal performance, and farm profits provides incentives for producers to manage operations in harmony with the local environment (page 261)."
And
"Net pens crate new underwater structures in local environments. These structures and their associated mooring systems provide three-dimensional habitat similar to engineered artificial reefs. Net-pen facilities can increase the total amount of habitat available and habitat complexity. Structures, such as pens and their associated anchoring systems, preclude access by trawlers to benthic ecosystems under and around farms. These ecosystems are therefore protected from the physical damage associated with destructive fishing practices (page 261)."
Benetti et al. (2010) provided a succinct set of farm site selection criteria and the rationale for each that addressed environmental (physical and biological), socio-economic and practical issues.
Marine finfish farms operate in dynamic environments where no one farm will be like another because of the variety of production gear used, farm size and disposition and seasonally changing climatic and oceanic conditions. We offer our assistance to organize farm design, siting and operation workshops and farm tours for the US Army Corps of Engineers of active operations.
Sincerely,
Jim Parsons
President
cc: Brianne McGuffie, HQUSACE Regulatory Program Manager

Center for Food Safety: Coalition Appeals EPA Permit Allowing Industrial Wastewater From Fish Farm in Gulf of Mexico

Full text: The Center for Food Safety issued the following news release:
On Friday, Center for Food Safety, the Don't Cage Our Ocean Coalition, and allies filed an appeal against the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency's (EPA) pollution discharge permit for Velella Epsilon, an industrial finfish aquaculture facility vying for construction permits in federal waters off of Sarasota, Florida. The EPA's permit will allow the facility to dump untreated wastewater directly into the surrounding ecosystems.
Groups filing the appeal, which include Center for Food Safety, Recirculating Farms Coalition, Friends of the Earth, Center for Biological Diversity, Food & Water Watch, Healthy Gulf, Suncoast Waterkeeper, and Tampa Bay Waterkeeper, argue that the permit violates the Clean Water Act (CWA), the National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA), and the Endangered Species Act (ESA).
The organizations released the following statement today:
"This permit should never have been issued. The EPA violated the law by failing to evaluate the potential dangers of the wastewater that this facility will release, such as contributing to harmful algal blooms (also often known as red tide) and pharmaceutical residues in the ocean and antibiotic resistance in people. By allowing this facility to pollute, the EPA has failed to protect the vulnerable Gulf ecosystem and the communities that rely on it. This needs to stop."
[Category: Food/Beverage]
MSTRUCK-7300758 MSTRUCK
