National Aquaculture Association Issues Public Comment on Fish &amp; Wildlife Service Proposed Rule

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]11 Mar 2020.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/national-aquaculture-association-issues-public/docview/2375706525/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=National+Aquaculture+Association+Issues+Public+Comment+on+Fish+%26amp%3Bamp%3B+Wildlife+Service+Proposed+Rule&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-03-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Targeted News Service
WASHINGTON, March 11 -- Jim Parsons, president of the National Aquaculture Association, Tallahassee, Florida, has issued a public comment on the Fish and Wildlife Service's proposed rule entitled "Migratory Bird Permits: Management of Double-crested Cormorants (Phalacrocorax auritus) throughout the United States". The comment was written on March 9, and posted on March 10, 2020:
* * *
Please accept the following comments from the National Aquaculture Association (NAA)/1 relative to an advance notice of proposed rulemaking concerning management of the double-crested cormorant (Phalacrocorax auritus) in the United States.
The NAA strongly supports the re-establishment of an Aquaculture Depredation Order (AQDO) as a reasonable and effective means for farmers utilizing outdoor production facilities, primarily ponds or flooded croplands, to deter consumption of their farm-raised fish or crawfish by double-crested cormorants for these reasons:
1. Double-crested cormorant predation of farm-raised fish causes significant crop loss and economic damage.
2. Oversight by two federal agencies, when provided by an AQDO, was effective in protecting double-crested cormorant populations and managing on-farm fish predation.
3. Double-crested cormorant populations increased during the period the AQDO and Public Resource Depredation Order were in effect.
4. The individual permit currently available significantly constrains farm-level control and management prescriptions that could be provided by the U.S. Department of Agriculture Wildlife Services under an AQDO.
Fish Predation
The double-crested cormorant is a large water bird that feeds mainly on fish and can consume approximately a pound of fish per day. Wires (2104) characterized the double-crested cormorant as being "...the most significant avian predator on fish in North America (page 25)" and noted 4.5-pound adult birds can consume 20 to 25 percent of their body mass daily (.9 to 1.1 pounds). Commercial fish ponds can be stocked at high densities ranging, for example, from 2,000 to 60,000 catfish per acre or 50,000 to almost 200,000 bait fish per acre. These efficient production practices make fish farms highly susceptible to bird predation, particularly by cormorants.
Considerable research has focused on double-crested cormorant predation upon farm-raised catfish in the State of Mississippi because of the size, scale and economic importance of catfish aquaculture. The analyses supported the establishment of an AQDO in 1998 and subsequent refinement of that research has occurred to document crop loss in subsequent years and to support the continuance of an AQDO as a successful management paradigm.
The research focus on Mississippi catfish should not be interpreted to mean farm-raised fish predation is unimportant to the production of baitfish, sportfish, ornamental fish (aquarium or water gardening) or to the stocking of farm-raised bait or sportfish for recreational fishing in other states. Ornamental fish, on a weight basis, may represent a farm-gate value of $20.00 or more per pound. Double-crested cormorants readily prey on colorful ornamental fish and will rapidly consume a pool of tropical ornamental fish before a farmer can react (Sandy Moore, Segrest Farms, personal communication; Craig Watson, Director - University of Florida, Tropical Aquaculture Laboratory, personal communication). A study conducted by US Department of Agriculture Wildlife Services demonstrated that losses on ornamental fish farms in central Florida due to fish-eating birds were as high as 30% (Avery et al. 1999). Barras (2007) summarized the variety of birds and types of farm-raised fish consumed by birds, including double-crested cormorants, in the Southern United States. A report entitled, Wildlife Services Program: Information on Activities to Manage Wildlife Damage (GAO 2001) stated:
"Fish-eating birds (e.g., cormorants, herons, egrets, and pelicans) can cause severe damage at aquaculture farms, eating catfish, crawfish, salmon, bass, trout, and ornamental fish. According to a USDA National Agricultural Statistics Service (NASS) survey of catfish producers from 15 states, 69 percent reported some wildlife-caused losses, with a financial loss of $12.5 million to wildlife predation in 1996 (page 8)."
Glahn and Brugger (1995) developed a bioenergetics model for wintering cormorants in the Mississippi Delta that relied on environmental factors, cormorant abundance, and prey consumption rates to estimate daily fish intake. Results from their model estimated approximately 900 metric tons, 20 million fish per winter, of catfish were consumed annually in 1989-1991 or 4% of the standing crop at a replacement cost of $2 million annually. Dorr et al. (2012) estimated approximately 1,560 metric tons of catfish were consumed annually in 2000-2001 and 2003-2004 representing an annual estimated economic loss of $5.6 million to $12 million. This increase was attributed to greater cormorant abundance in the Delta and subsequent increased foraging days and depredation of higher value fish from food fish ponds. We note that these estimates underestimate the loss to the fish farming community as they apply only to Mississippi and don't include cost of bird management or multiplier effects to on-farm and off-farm economies.
Since those studies, Mississippi farmers have adopted a split-pond production system that reduces pond surface area but increases pond stocking density (Tucker and Kingsbury 2009). To evaluate double-crested cormorant predation upon this new production system, a recent predator-prey bioenergetics model (i.e., quantification of cormorant abundance, distribution, and foraging ecology on catfish aquaculture to estimate the total biomass of catfish consumed) estimated 4.2 and 5 million double-crested cormorant foraging days occurred during winters 2016-2018 with an average of 33% of the bird's diet consisting of catfish, or 558.1 and 739.5 metric tons of catfish consumed by cormorants in winters 2016-2018 (Christie 2019).
Additional economic impact analysis related to the bioenergetics model has been published or is in review for publication (Engle et al. in review; Christie et al. 2020). The authors found fish-eating birds result in additional costs on farms as catfish farmers attempt to scare birds off their ponds. In spite of efforts to scare birds, cormorants still consume farmed fish that result in additional negative economic effects associated with reduced farm revenue. Losses to birds in the absence of double-created cormorant management would be substantially greater. The costs to scare birds and the value of fish losses were found to have increased over time (all values were adjusted for inflation). This research will be reporting:
* Catfish farmers that contributed data to the analyses reported a per-acre cost to scare birds of $285 + $159 per acre, making bird-scaring costs the 5th-greatest cost in catfish production. The greatest components of bird-scaring costs were manpower (39%) and truck expenses (34%). The annualized industry-wide value of lost catfish sales revenue to cormorants averaged $47.2 million, ranging from $25.8 million to $65.4 million, depending upon predation levels in any given year. The combined total of negative direct economic effects of the annualized costs of scaring birds and the value of fish lost to cormorants averaged $64.7 million. Including multiplier effects, the effect on total economic output averaged $70.8 million and on economic value added $85.0 million annually.
* To better evaluate the effect at the farm-level, the negative effects of cormorant predation (both costs to scare cormorants and the additional fish losses) were modeled in 27 farm size/production strategies. Removing the negative effects of cormorant predation improved profitability across all scenarios, with percentage increases in profitability ranging from 4% to 23%, with the greatest percentage improvements observed on the smallest farm scenarios and resulted in all but one of the previously unprofitable farm size/production strategies becoming profitable.
* Improvements in profitability reduce financial risk on catfish farms both by reducing costs of production and providing a greater margin of safety in the event of market price decreases. With lower costs of production, farms remain profitable at lower market prices, reducing market price risk.
* Cormorant predation also has led to increased inefficiencies in the way farms are managed. As stated by one respondent, "We have changed how we manage our entire farm due to birds, not efficiency; we schedule seining of ponds with the greatest bird pressure first; anyone going into town drives by ponds with birds on the way in and comes back a different way."
Unlike other forms of agriculture, losses of catfish to federally protected avian predators such as double-crested cormorants are not covered under federal programs that were created to mitigate some portion of these types of losses. Catfish fall through cracks between the several crop loss programs managed by the USDA Farm Service Agency in terms of coverage for losses due to cormorants: Livestock Indemnity Protection, the Emergency Assistance for Livestock, Honeybees and Farm-raised Fish Program, and the Noninsured Crop Disaster Assistance Program.
As mentioned, considerable and intensive analysis has focused on double-crested cormorant induced direct and indirect losses in the State of Mississippi. Recent studies concerning scaup (Aythya spp.) predation on Arkansas baitfish farms can be inferred to estimate potential losses from double-crested cormorants. These estimates are probably low because scaup are somewhat specialized in their diets with various animals from classes Insecta, Malacostraca, and Bivalvia as important foods and may only prefer farm-raised baitfish during cold winters (mean daily temperature of 40 degree F) because of the birds' increased energy demands, prey availability, and ease of capture, or some combination of these (Clements et al. 2020). Based upon a cold winter analysis, (December-February, 2017-2018), Clements et al. (2018) reported as many as 51% of the scaup sampled contained baitfish (i.e., golden shiners (Notemigonus crysoleucas)) and individual scaup can consume about 0.5 pound of fish or over 300 individual golden shiners per day. Based on the number of scaup present on a farm and fish consumption during this winter, losses per acre on ponds containing scaup averaged 37,600 golden shiners (estimated range = 16,600 to 58,700 fish per acre). Although other types of birds also eat farmed fish, the average amount of fish consumed by scaup alone corresponded to losses of about 25% of the fish crop. This percentage varied across baitfish farm ponds, where losses of the fish crop were as low as about 8% and as high as 59%.
Losses of baitfish to avian predators occur despite intensive efforts by baitfish farmers to scare birds from their farms. This study found that baitfish farmers spend approximately $250 per acre each year to attempt to scare avian predators from their farms. Losses of golden shiners eaten by scaup resulted in lost revenue of $50 per acre in a cold winter. The combined economic effect of lost revenue from fish losses and the expense of scaring birds decreased overall profit margins by $251 per acre (years with few birds) to $300 per acre in colder years when birds arrive to feed on baitfish on farms. For the median size of golden shiner farm of 800 acres, this represents economic losses of $200,800 per farm in years with few birds and $240,000 per farm in years with high bird pressure.
In addition to direct predation, double-crested cormorants can transmit: 1) parasitic trematodes into catfish production ponds that infect farm-raised catfish and render the processed fillet unmarketable (Overstreet and Curran 2004; Rosser et al. 2016), 2) parasitic flukes that kill juvenile fish (Griffin et al. 2014), and 3) bacterial pathogens (including a hypervirulent strain of Aeromonas hydrophila) that kill farmed fish, incur treatment costs or slow fish growth and increase production costs (Taylor 1992; Cunningham et al. 2018). The economic effects of these introductions have not yet been quantified or estimated.
Aquaculture Depredation Order
The AQDO represented smart government through collaboration between the US Fish and Wildlife Service (FWS) biological experts and the on-farm assistance provided by the US Department of Agriculture Wildlife Services (WS). The AQDO allowed commercial freshwater fish producers in Alabama, Arkansas, Florida, Georgia, Kentucky, Louisiana, Minnesota, Mississippi, North Carolina, Oklahoma, South Carolina, Tennessee, and Texas to take double-crested cormorants when the birds were found committing or about to commit depredation on aquaculture stocks. However, farmers are first required to implement non-lethal techniques as prescribed by WS as a precondition to being permitted to use lethal take. Farms are required to report the number of birds killed during the permit period (Barras and Godwin 2005; Dorr and Fielder 2017). Most of the farms affected are family-owned and located in rural areas. Fish farmers do not perceive the shooting of double-crested cormorants as sport or recreation and must invest considerable labor, equipment (vehicles, propane cannons, pyrotechnics, effigies and other nonlethal devices) and fuel (gasoline/diesel or propane) to deter predation by non-lethal means (Gorenzel et al. 1994). The frank opinion of fish farmers is: "It's just a pain in the ass, and I don't see it getting any better from the all the time I've been out here (page 175)" (King 2013).
Research indicates the AQDO was working as well. Burr (2019) conducted aerial surveys of cormorants using catfish aquaculture versus natural waterbodies in Mississippi from 2015-2017. During the 2015 survey period, catfish producers were allowed to use lethal control against cormorants without a federal permit under the AQDO, in the 2016 surveys, the AQDO was ceased, and only non-lethal means of harassment could be legally used to reduce cormorant presence. During the 2017 survey producers were allowed limited lethal take, under a depredation permit system.
Burr (2019) found that the proportion of cormorants using aquaculture in 2015 was 55%, climbed to 88% in 2016, and was intermediate at 79% in 2017. Burr's findings show lethal control may be influencing how cormorants distribute themselves between the two available foraging options (aquaculture vs. natural water bodies). Removing the AQDO option may have resulted in habituation to non-lethal methods and an overall reduced risk perceived by cormorants resulting in increased use of aquaculture facilities to forage. Arkansas bait fish farmers reported during the 2016 winter a higher proportion of immature double-crested cormorants that were far more difficult to haze using nonlethal techniques (Jamie Anderson, IF Anderson Farms, personal communication).
Population Growth Data from a variety of peer reviewed papers and published sources (Weseloh et al. 2002; Wires and Cuthbert 2006; Adkins et al. 2014; Dorr et al. 2014: Wires 2014; Dorr and Fielder 2017) indicates double-crested cormorant populations in North America have increased during the tenure of the Aquaculture Depredation Order (1998 to 2016) and Public Resource Depredation Order (2003 to 2016). This is exemplified by population trend data from the American Breeding Bird Survey copied and pasted at right/2 which estimated an increase of 93% for the period 1966-2011 (Sauer et al. 2013). Wires (2014) commented "Well over a million cormorants are estimated to reside annually in North America, and a take of 40,000 to 50,000 birds yearly likely represents less than 5 percent of the population. Clearly, the cormorant is currently not in any danger of becoming extinct (pages 275-276)."/3
Overall population expansion has resulted in recolonization to historic locations (Ayers et al. 2019), re-establishment of breeding populations in Indiana, Ohio, Iowa, Arkansas, Illinois, Minnesota, Mississippi and Missouri (Wires and Cuthbert 2006), range expansion of birds from northwestern Mexico to Southern California (Adkins et al. 2014) and changing migration and wintering patterns for this growing population as a result of commercial aquaculture in the Southeastern United States (Wires and Cuthbert 2006; King et al. 2010; King et al. 2012). As summarized by summarized by Ayers et al. (2019), "These patterns suggest enormous potential for Double-crested Cormorants to repopulate, recolonize, and establish new colonies in North America (page 10)."
Individual Migratory Bird Depredation Permits Constrain Management Options Currently, fish farmers throughout the country are required to contact WS for technical assistance in the management of depredating wildlife and birds. The WS biologists complete an evaluation (Form 37) that describes the incident and documents recommendations for nonlethal and lethal management options. When lethal take is recommended, the Form 37 is forwarded by the applicant with an application fee to an FWS Regional Office to acquire a Federal Migratory Bird Depredation Permit./4
Farms are required to report the number of birds killed during the permit period. Individual permits have been problematical for farms in that:
1. Insufficient birds are allocated to a farm because the national allocation is insufficient or year-to-year appearance of birds on a farm is quite variable.
2. Individual permits may be issued by FWS regional offices on a seasonal rather than calendar basis.
3. Individual permit provisions currently prohibit the use of lead rifle or pellet ammunition.
4. FWS Regional Offices may prohibit the use of rifles.
5. Individual permit provisions currently prohibit the use of decoys.
6. An AQDO should be expanded to include additional states.
Bird Allocation The National Aquaculture Association requests the re-establishment of the AQDO to provide flexibility by WS biologists and farm managers to respond to year-to-year variations in fish predation by double-crested cormorants. Nearly 18 years of double-crested cormorant management under the AQDO, Public Resource Depredation Order and individual Migratory Bird Depredation Permits have yielded adequate management options for farms and an expanding double-crested cormorant population. Since 2017 when only individual permits were available, farms have experienced inadequate allocation of birds for individual permits and time-consuming delays to amend permits. An 800 water acre Wisconsin baitfish farm was recently allocated two birds and a 1,650 water acres Texas food fish farm was initially allocated 10 birds for six weeks when individual permits became available. Complicating national allocation analysis is the year-to-year variability in double-crested cormorant predation that was accommodated by the AQDO but not with individual permits that include specific allocation limits. Dorr et al. (2012) reported variable year-to-year distribution of double-crested cormorants between interior and river associated roosting sites that resulted in crop losses that varied significantly from year-to-year. Currently, Arkansas and Texas fish farms are reporting little to no double-crested cormorant feeding on some farms while at the same time reporting high densities of birds feeding on other farms. Bird presence is completely unpredictable or explainable from farm-to-farm and year-to-year (Neal Ford Anderson, IF Anderson Farms, personal communication; David Dunn, Dunn's Fish Farm, personal communication; Jim Ekstrom, Ekstrom Enterprises, personal communication; Mike Freeze, Keo Fish Farm, personal communication; Dave Gollon, Gollon Bait and Fish Farm, personal communication).
Seasonal Individual Permits
The National Aquaculture Association requests the re-establishment of the AQDO to avoid challenges posed by individual permits issued on a seasonal rather than calendar year basis by FWS Regional Offices. The expansion of double-crested cormorants into historical and novel locations and the establishment of breeding colonies in historical and novel locations results in year-round farmed fish predation. Seasonal permits limit effective cormorant management of fish predation by the presence of double-crested cormorants throughout a calendar year.
Lead Rifle or Pellet Ammunition
The National Aquaculture Association requests the re-establishment of the AQDO to allow the WS to evaluate and authorize the use of lead rifle and pellet gun ammunition to manage double-crested cormorants or the authorization to use lead rifle or pellet gun ammunition under the existing individual permit. The Environmental Assessment for Issuing Depredation Permits for Double-crested Cormorant Management (FWS 2017) includes an in-depth analysis of the use of lead ammunition for double- crested cormorant management (pages 32-33) noting rifles and pellet guns are used to shoot birds in specific instances (i.e., shooting when mixed species are present, near habitable structures, or when people are present) and that birds downed by firearms are collected and buried as required by permit. The FWS Division of Migratory Bird Management concluded the use of lead rifle or pellet ammunition presents low direct, indirect or cumulative environmental effects because of limited lead use, burial of downed birds, environmental risk is substantially less than that posed by shotgun ammunition, and use of lead rifle or pellet ammunition over water presents a low risk of lead related water quality effects (pages 45-46). These conclusions were reflected in the standard conditions proposed for Migratory Bird Depredation Permits (page 76) which included the use of nontoxic shotgun ammunition but did not require nontoxic rifle or pellet ammunition.
Use of Rifles
The National Aquaculture Association requests the re-establishment of the AQDO to allow the use of rifles as authorized by WS biologists or the addition of rifles as a management tool under the existing individual permit. Currently, rifles are not allowed under individual permits issued by certain FWS Regional Offices. As noted, rifles are effective under specific instances (i.e., shooting when mixed species are present, near structures, or when people are present). And lead rifle ammunition presents low direct, indirect or cumulative environmental effects because of limited lead use, burial of downed birds, environmental risk is substantially less than that posed by shotgun ammunition, and use of lead rifle ammunition over water presents a low risk of lead related water quality effects (FWS 2017).
Use of Decoys
The National Aquaculture Association requests the re-establishment of the AQDO to allow the use of decoys as authorized by WS biologists or the addition of decoys as a management tool under the existing individual permit. The Environmental Assessment for Issuing Depredation Permits for Double-crested Cormorant Management (FWS 2017) did not discuss the use of decoys but did prohibit their use in the standard conditions proposed for Migratory Bird Depredation Permits (page 76). Double-crested cormorant decoys are a valuable tool for on-farm management. As noted in Dorr et al. (2016) in a publication developed to inform wildlife damage management, "Cormorants respond well to both floating and silhouette decoys, which can make shooting more effective and reduce non-target take (page 8)." The use of decoys allows for the removal of individual cormorants that have become habituated to the use of hazing techniques. These habituated individuals act as live decoys, attracting additional cormorants to a facility and their removal actually decreases the overall take of cormorants on an individual facility. The farming community is desirous of being as effective as humanly possible in managing depredating birds and avoiding the killing of non-target birds.
Additional States Should be Added to an Aquaculture Depredation Order The National Aquaculture Association requests that a re-established AQDO encompass all 48 states where pond production of fish or crawfish occurs. The 2018 Census of Aquaculture (USDA 2018) reports 1,450 farms used 32,017 ponds encompassing 142,049 acres to produce fish and 460 farms utilized 94,880 acres of flooded cropland to produce crawfish. Except for Delaware and Nevada, 48 states used ponds or flooded cropland for fish or crawfish production.
Re-Establish the Public Resource Depredation Order
The National Aquaculture Association requests re-establishing the Public Resource Depredation Order to create a science-based population management plan to protect farm-raised fish and crawfish. Doublecrested cormorants breed in the Great Lakes region and in the fall migrate to the Southeastern United States to overwinter and prey on farmed and wild fish (King et al. 2012; Wires 2014; Craig et al. 2016; FWS 2017). As already noted, the number of double-crested cormorants is increasing and birds are, or will be as the population continues to expand, occupying or colonizing historic or novel locations as migrants or residents. An integrated bird management plan is necessary for migratory or resident breeding colonies and populations to reduce bird-human conflict much as was demonstrated by the successful 13-year, concurrent Aquaculture and Public Resource Depredation Orders.
Thank you for the opportunity to contribute to proposed rulemaking and the potential expansion of cormorant management options for the US aquaculture community and public agencies. Please do not hesitate to contact us for copies of cited references.
Sincerely,
Jim Parsons
President

Sarasota commissioners speak against Gulf fish farm proposal

Author: Fanning, Timothy

Publication info: The News Press ; Fort Myers, Fla. [Fort Myers, Fla]05 Feb 2020: A.1.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/sarasota-commissioners-speak-against-gulf-fish/docview/2350807584/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Sarasota+commissioners+speak+against+Gulf+fish+farm+proposal&amp;title=The+News+Press&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-02-05&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Fanning%2C+Timothy&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+News+Press&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: With a deadline to voice opposition looming, Sarasota city commissioners agreed late Monday to send a letter that condemns a controversial precedent-setting fish farm planned for waters off the coast of Sarasota to the federal regulators who will decide whether to authorize it.
Commissioners have a "strong and formal opposition" to a pilot project by Kampachi Farms that plans to anchor a chain-link mesh pen offshore of Southwest Florida, according to a draft letter addressed to Kip Tyler, an environmental engineer for the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. The project aims to raise 20,000 almaco jack about 45 miles southwest of Sarasota County.
As part of the EPA's permitting process to determine if discharges from the fish will adversely affect the water, the federal agency had been collecting feedback. The deadline to submit formal comments is Tuesday.
The letter, drafted by Mayor Jen Ahearn-Koch, will also be sent to Sarasota County commissioners and Mote Marine Laboratory, which would provide fingerling almaco jack spawned and raised at its land-based Mote Aquaculture Research Park.
The letter, signed by Ahearn-Koch, expresses concerns that the discharge of nitrogen and phosphorous would affect waters known for harmful algal blooms. Citing the Gulf's sensitivity to red tide, the letter goes on to say that commissioners further believe that adding those discharges to "our warm Gulf waters is too risky and dangerous, even on a trial basis."
As part of the farm's monitoring requirements, Kampachi must test monthly for nitrogen and phosphorus levels, among other discharges near the net pen. The EPA has also said that the project, dubbed Velella Epsilon, won't have a detrimental environmental impact.
The red tide outbreak in 2018 left Florida's coastlines devastated — affecting quality of life of residents, tourists and the economic vitality of Sarasota's tourism and related businesses, the letter says.
"As elected officials, we must protect the health, welfare and safety of our constituents," Ahearn-Koch wrote in the letter.
Although commissioners generally support the pursuit of aquaculture, it "objects to the experimental nature of the Kampachi Farms project."
A view from inside Kampachi Farms' Velella Gamma-test net pen in Kona, Hawaii.
Jeff Milisen
CREDIT: Timothy Fanning

____________________________________________________________

Supporters, opponents debate Gulf fish farm

Author: Fanning, Timothy

Publication info: The News Press ; Fort Myers, Fla. [Fort Myers, Fla]30 Jan 2020: A.5.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/supporters-opponents-debate-gulf-fish-farm/docview/2348177341/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Supporters%2C+opponents+debate+Gulf+fish+farm&amp;title=The+News+Press&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-01-30&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=Fanning%2C+Timothy&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+News+Press&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: SARASOTA – Supporters and opponents of a potential precedent-setting fish farm planned for federally controlled waters off the coast of Sarasota made their cases Tuesday to federal regulators who will decide whether to authorize it.
Their comments came during a hearing at Mote Marine Laboratory in Sarasota as part of the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency's permitting process to determine if discharges from the fish will adversely affect the water.
Opponents of the proposal by a Hawaii-based company to anchor a chain-link mesh pen offshore of Southwest Florida to raise 20,000 almaco jack about 45 miles southwest of Sarasota, decried recent environmental scars on coastal Florida.
They also expressed concerns that the discharge of nitrogen and phosphorous would affect waters known for harmful algal blooms.
Meanwhile, proponents argued that many of the criticisms of offshore fish farming were based on outdated practices that were abandoned years ago.
Supporters also argued that the proposal would be a vital first step for much-needed data for the future of offshore aquaculture in the United States.
Kampachi Farms' pilot project, dubbed Velella Epsilon, is expected to produce about 88,000 pounds of fish.
Sixty people pre-registered to comment at the hearing and well over 120 people came to listen.
Pauleen Home, a resident of Lakewood Ranch, spoke against the pilot project.
Home said that although the farm has made impressive strides in aquaculture farming and research in the colder waters off the coast of Hawaii, the floating net pen is the "wrong solution" for the shallower, warmer waters in the Gulf of Mexico. Temperatures can reach up to 90 degrees in the summertime.
Kavan Main is manager for the Marine and freshwater aquaculture research program at Mote and has led the nonprofit's aquaculture research programs since 2001. Main did not speak on behalf of Mote and said that much of the criticism of aquaculture is based on outdated practices that were abandoned years ago. Main also said that best management practices have been developed to improve aquaculture.
"Time is of the essence in developing U.S marine aquaculture," Main said. "We are far behind other countries. We need to do this in a manner that is both environmentally sustainable and provides info that we need."
As part of the farm's monitoring requirements, Kampachi must test monthly for nitrogen and phosphorus levels, among other discharges near the net. As part of the EPA's Ocean Discharge Criteria for the farm, the project won't have a detrimental environmental impact.
The farm will have graduate students from the University of Miami monitoring the waters surrounding the farm, Neil Sims, CEO of Kampachi Farms told the Naples Daily news last week.
In addition to Florida Sea Grant, Sarasota's Mote Marine Laboratory is a stakeholder in the project. Mote will provide fingerling almaco jack spawned and raised at its land-based Mote Aquaculture Research Park for the Kampachi demonstration project.
The net pen would be 17 meters in diameter and 7meters deep, and located 40 meters below the surface. It would be anchored to the bottom and tended by a feed barge that would be tethered to the side of the net pen.
The fish would grow to market size within 12months.
At the hearing, Justin Bloom, the founder and board member of the Suncoast Waterkeeper, took a more even-handed criticisms of the project.
Although the nonprofit recognized the need for off-shore aquaculture and for a reliable source of healthy seafood and saw the potential for such facilities, he said that "federal law calls for a more thorough review and analysis in the form of a full environmental impact study of the potential environmental and socio-economic impacts from the project."
Dennis Peters, Velella Epsilon and permit coordinator for Kampachi Farms, said that he was not surprised by the comments expressed on Tuesday — most of it centering on the possibility of additional fish farming projects in the Gulf.
Peters noted any additional aquaculture venture would be subject for additional permits.
"I think a lot of them were confused and were focusing on the commercial potential verses the real benefits of the data that can be provided through the pilot project," Peters said. "It's not a foregone conclusion to say that this is going to go."
Should the EPA greenlight the project, Kampachi Farms must also get permits from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers because of the need to anchor in federal waters.
Mary Walker, the EPA's regional administrator, will make a determination after the Feb. 4 comment deadline. If her determination is substantially unchanged, Walker will notify anyone who has submitted written comments.
Anyone who filed comments on the draft permit or participated in the public hearing can file an appeal within 30 days after Walker files the notice.
Before the hearing, more than a dozen demonstrators stood at the corner of Ken Thompson and John Ringling Parkways and along the sidewalks.
Most had environmental concerns, contending that chemicals flowing out of the pen could disrupt and pollute the natural ecosystem.
Paul Zajicek, the executive director of the National Aquaculture Association, joined the protesters, hoping to learn and listen to their concerns.
Zajicek noted that since the 1970s, the EPA has held authority to regulate discharges from fish farms under several iterations of the Clean Water Act. More recently, environmental groups sought EPA re-evaluation of the Clean Water Act standards applied to aquaculture.
Zajicek said that the evidence is not yet conclusive on whether or not an offshore fish farm will cause red tide. That will be determined after the pilot project is completed, he said.
Aquaculture farmers, like Kampachi Farms, have been completely transparent and are "just as concerned as they are" about nutrient pollution and other environmental concerns, Zajicek said.
A view from inside Kampachi Farms' Velella Gamma-test net pen in Kona, Hawaii.
Jeff Milisen
CREDIT: Timothy Fanning

____________________________________________________________

Proposed Gulf fish farm spurs controversy

Author: Schneider, Karl

Publication info: The News Press ; Fort Myers, Fla. [Fort Myers, Fla]25 Jan 2020: A.6.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/proposed-gulf-fish-farm-spurs-controversy/docview/2344506695/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Proposed+Gulf+fish+farm+spurs+controversy%3A+EPA+hearing+to+address+environmental+concerns&amp;title=The+News+Press&amp;issn=&amp;date=2020-01-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.6&amp;au=Schneider%2C+Karl&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+News+Press&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: A proposed finfish farm in federally controlled waters will be in the spotlight during a public hearing in Sarasota on Jan. 28. The farm will be the first of its kind in the Gulf of Mexico and discussions are expected to revolve around environmental concerns.
The hearing is part of the Environmental Protection Agency's permitting process to determine if discharges from the fish will adversely affect the water.
"America imports over 90% of seafood that we eat," said Neil Sims, CEO of Kampachi Farms. "If folks are really concerned about environmental issues, then they should be focused on growing seafood locally rather than exporting our ecological footprint."
Kampachi Farms is the Hawaii-based company proposing the finfish farm dubbed Velella Epsilon. The project is proposed as a pilot program to test the viability of fish farms in the Gulf. A single net pen will raise about 20,000 almaco jack roughly 45 miles southwest of Sarasota. The farm is expected to produce about 88,000 pounds of fish.
"The hearing will begin with a brief presentation by the EPA and a neutral process facilitator, followed by oral comments from interested persons," the EPA's public notice of the hearing says.
Among the environmental concerns are the discharge of nitrogen and phosphorus into waters known to harbor harmful algal blooms.
"The environment and community would be paying a direct price from toxins and pollution released into the waters," said Rosanna Marie Neil, policy director for the Northwest Atlantic Marine Alliance. "I believe it would make harmful algal blooms worse as there could be higher amounts of nitrogen released into the ocean and that can make red tide worse."
As part of the farm's monitoring requirements, Kampachi must test monthly for nitrogen and phosphorus levels, among other discharges, near the net. As part of the EPA's Ocean Discharge Criteria for the farm, the agency "determined that sufficient information exists to conclude that the discharge from the facility would not cause unreasonable degradation of the marine environment ... "
"There is no doubt that it would exacerbate red tide and algal bloom," said Hallie Templeton, senior oceans campaigner for Friends of the Earth. "A lot of reason for the blooms is increased nutrients in water such as agricultural runoff, mostly human caused. Sometimes algal blooms can be caused naturally, but they're exacerbated by industrial uses."
The farm will have graduate students from the University of Miami monitoring the waters surrounding the farm, Sims said.
"Not only will there be no significant impact, it won't even be detectable," Sims said of the discharges coming from the net pen. "We have monitoring around our farms here in Kona. We produce 500 tons of fish in a year, and you couldn't tell the difference between up current and down current of the pens."
While the public hearing in Sarasota is based on pollution discharges from the pilot program, there is concern among some that the net pen will not be able to weather the storms that roll through the Gulf.
"The Gulf of Mexico gets looked at a lot as calm, but because of its shallowness, it ends up becoming a washing machine," Jason DeLaCruz said. "It can sometimes be far rougher than people give it credit for."
DeLaCruz founded Wild Seafood Company in Johns Pass near St. Petersburg. He's in the wholesale business and runs a bait and tackle shop on the pass. He's worried Kampachi's net pen will be in water too shallow for it to withstand the Gulf's more violent weather systems.
"(The project) may be a great thing," he said. "We certainly need more ways to feed people, but the ocean will be the answer in the long term. Maybe the answer is it's just not our coast where the shelf goes super far out to reach deep water."
DeLaCruz and others are concerned that if fish escape from the pen, it could cause genetic problems with wild populations.
"Especially when this happens on a large scale, (escaped fish) can interbreed with wild fish and compromise their genetic integrity," Rosanna Neil said. "They can sometimes create competition for food and they can wreak havoc on the ecosystem because they are not identical to wild fish."
Sims acknowledged that nature is unpredictable.
"We are going to engineer this to be able to withstand the sorts of storms that frequent the Gulf," he said, "but there's no absolute guarantees that anything happens in the water. Working in the ocean is managing risk and from our past experience I think we are comfortable managing around this."
The almaco jack stock going into the pen is of the same native gene pool of those found in the Gulf, Sims said. The parents of the fish to be raised in the pen were collected from Sarasota.
The pen is designed to be submersible to about 50 feet below the surface and move with the waves and currents.
"One of the innovations we are demonstrating with Velella Epsilon is we will have this on a swivel mooring so the pen will move around with the current in an arc rather than being fixed at one point, and that further reduces the potential for any impact," Sims said.
The point of the demonstration pen is to collect information.
"We assure this can grow in a way that has minimal impact," Sims said. "We can only do that if we have a demonstration and people can actually see what the impacts are and the potential (of the farm)."
Ahead of the public hearing, groups are organizing a demonstration against the fish farm project on Tuesday.
"We're hoping to raise awareness, educate and send a message to the EPA," Templeton said. "We're going to show that there is a community opposition and it will not go untested and that people care about what's happening on their coastline."
Demonstration organizers include the Center for Biological Diversity, Friends of the Earth, Healthy Gulf, Northwest Atlantic Marine Alliance, Sierra Club, Suncoast Waterkeeper and others.
The demonstration is from 4:30 to 5:30 p.m. at the corner of Ken Thompson and John Ringling Parkways and along the sidewalk. The hearing begins at 5:30 p.m. at the Wave Center, Mote's main laboratory building at 1600 Ken Thompson Parkway.
Timothy Fanning, staff writer for the Sarasota Herald Tribune, contributed to this report. Karl Schneider is an environment reporter. Follow him on Facebook and Twitter: @karlstartswithk, email him at kschneider@gannett.com

____________________________________________________________

Offshore fish farm idea may cause fuss

Author: Schneider, Karl

Publication info: The News Press ; Fort Myers, Fla. [Fort Myers, Fla]09 Dec 2019: A.1.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/offshore-fish-farm-idea-may-cause-fuss/docview/2322617091/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Offshore+fish+farm+idea+may+cause+fuss&amp;title=The+News+Press&amp;issn=&amp;date=2019-12-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Schneider%2C+Karl&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+News+Press&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: A Hawaii-based company is planning an offshore finfish farm in federal waters off the coast of Southwest Florida.
Kampachi Farms has proposed a pilot-scale marine aquaculture facility named Velella Epsilon to be built in the Gulf of Mexico 45 miles south of Sarasota.
"I think there is a pressing global need to expand food production," said Neil Sims, co-founder of Kampachi Farms. "A United Nations high level panel on oceans said one of the five key steps to address the climate crisis is to start to shift from terrestrial agriculture to marine agriculture."
The proposed farm will raise 88,000 pounds of almaco jack fish each year, the same amount caught by commercial fishers in Florida each year. Sims said the Velella Epsilon project will consist of a demonstration pen where the farmed fish will be held. The pen will measure 20 feet deep and 50 feet across. It is able to be submerged about 7 meters, or 20 feet.
"We would hope that if we were to get (a) permit by February or March next year, we will have fish in the water by July or August," Sims said. "It takes a lot of prep and it might be six months after permits."
The U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, working with the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers and the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, issued a draft environmental assessment for the project.
"On August 30, 2019, EPA Region 4 provided the public a 30-day comment period for a draft National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permit for the Kampachi Farms," EPA spokeswoman Dawn Harris Young wrote in an email. "During the 30-day public comment period, EPA received a significant number of comments. The EPA is currently reviewing the comments received and is evaluating the need for a public hearing and potential extension of the public comment period."
Environmental groups have said that the public comment period was not long enough and hope that the EPA opens a public hearing.
"We're watchdogging this closely," said Hallie Templeton, the senior oceans campaigner for the advocacy group Friends of the Earth. "We have urged the EPA to hold public input; we'll see what happens. There has been written comment, but we have not heard anything out of the agency."
Templeton said the farm poses a risk to the environment through the discharge of excess nutrients into the Gulf and other concerns.
"Net pens are free flowing exchange, and anything in a net pen can just leech into surrounding waterway," she said. "Fish waste and excess fish feed go into existing environment. There's no way that could be a win, it could not be beneficial. They say could be diluted, but dilution is not solution to the pollution."
Sims said there has been monitoring around Kampachi's other offshore pens that show no impact on water quality between up current and down current.
"Nutrients are rapidly assimilated into the ecosystem," he said. "The red tide issue found in Florida is usually very close to shore, and there's a lot of nutrients running off land. That's part of why we are doing this 40 miles offshore where there is good water exchange."
A large Florida red tide bloom caused massive fish kills and respiratory irritations last year and a smaller bloom is currently on the coast of Southwest Florida.
The EPA's draft assessment noted Florida's red tides, and acknowledged excess nutrients from fish farms can promote growth, but says, "(the) permit is being issued with conditions to monitor the discharge and protect water quality."
Kampachi's other Velella projects have all been monitored and were sort of test runs leading up to a larger scale operation, Sims said. After the fish pen in the Gulf is built, Sims hopes to move the company forward, but this worries environmentalists.
"The biggest thing I take issue with is calling this a pilot project," said Marianne Cufone, an environmental attorney and executive director of Recirculating Farms. "I get it's meant to be a test for open fish farming in (the) gulf, but the volume of fish is appalling for a pilot project, it's more like industrial farming."
Cufone said she doesn't understand how there could be no impact with a large operation, and Templeton expressed concerns that moving forward with Velella Epsilon would open the door for larger farms.
Because there is not any fish farming going on in the United States' federal waterways, Templeton said, "this is prying open the door for the industry."
Cufone said she and others are part of a lawsuit challenging NOAA to put regulations in place and she expects to have arguments in January.
"This is meant to be a tester for future industrial-scale projects," she said. "It's not a secret this project is meant to open the door to fish farms in the Gulf of Mexico."
Sims said this project is "about nurturing what we want out of the ocean and figuring out how to do this."
"We would expect that going forward any commercial project will have monitoring requirements," he said. "We think that it's important to share that info with the public."
To Sims, offshore aquaculture is an opportunity.
"We are scientists and conservationists," he said. "There is an economic incentive to drive the ecological imperative: We need to be getting more food from the sea."
A view from inside the Velella Beta-test net pen in Kona, Hawaii, with the fish in the foreground, the Aquapod net pen frame, and the attendant vessel the S.S. Machias, which was attached to the net pen array.
Jeff Milisen
CREDIT: Karl Schneider

Florida Agriculture Commissioner Fried Issues Testimony on Sustainable Offshore Aquaculture

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]16 Oct 2019.

http://proxy.library.jhu.edu/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/florida-agriculture-commissioner-fried-issues/docview/2306218431/se-2?accountid=11752

Abstract: None available.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Asoutheastnews&amp;atitle=Florida+Agriculture+Commissioner+Fried+Issues+Testimony+on+Sustainable+Offshore+Aquaculture&amp;title=Targeted+News+Service&amp;issn=&amp;date=2019-10-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Targeted+News+Service&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Florida Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services issued the following testimony by Commissioner Nicole "Nikki" Fried regarding the Senate Commerce, Science and Transportation Committee, in advance of the committee's hearing on sustainable offshore aquaculture:
In Florida, aquaculture is a $77 million industry, with over 700 aquaculture leases covering 2,700 submerged acres; the industry produces over 1,500 varieties of fish, plants, crustaceans, mollusks, corals, reptiles, and other marine products for food and non-food markets. Florida is one of the nation's top states for fresh seafood production, supporting 14,000 jobs. Florida also ranks 4th in the nation for shellfish production, with more than ten certified shellfish processing facilities in the state.
Commissioner Fried's testimony can be read in full below, and was also shared on Twitter.
Statement of the Honorable Nicole "Nikki" FriedU.S. Senate Committee on Commerce, Science, and TransportationHearing: Feeding America: Making SustainableOffshore Aquaculture a RealityOctober 16, 2019
"Chairman Wicker, Ranking Member Cantwell, and members of the Committee, thank you for convening this important hearing to discuss sustainable offshore aquaculture and the need for streamlined federal regulations to support this developing industry and ultimately, our nation's food security.
"Agriculture is Florida's second largest industry, with an economic impact of $132 billion in our state. Our 47,000 farms support two million workers and export $4 billion in commodities to 164 nations - feeding our neighbors, our communities, and the world. Aquaculture is Florida's most diverse agribusiness with over 1,000 aquaculturists producing the greatest variety of aquatic species in the nation, with an estimated 1,500 species or varieties of food and aquarium fish, shellfish, aquatic plants, mollusks, crustaceans, and reptiles grown in the state.
"In addition to the growth of this industry in Florida, aquaculture around the globe continues to increase in scale and diversity in order to meet our growing population's need for safe, affordable, and healthy protein. While the offshore food fish sector remains only a small fraction of all aquaculture production in the U.S., the nation's seafood trade deficit continues to grow. The production of domestic aquaculture products is critical for the nation's food security and economy.
"While offshore aquaculture has been critiqued for environmental impacts in past decades, significant technological and scientific innovations have made it possible for these farms to operate with minimal risk to the environment. Advances in fish husbandry, feed composition and genetics, along with drastically improved cage engineering, have now sufficiently addressed the negative perceptions of offshore aquaculture that have prohibited development of the nation's industry in the past. Through proper facility siting and the application of modern, responsible production methods, the U.S. can become a model for sustainable aquaculture production and promote the development of a responsible offshore aquaculture industry that will prove itself as a reliable source of domestic protein and as a good steward of the marine environment.
"However, for offshore aquaculture to develop, a clearly outlined federal permitting process and framework is necessary. For years, aquaculture companies have sought to bring farms and jobs to the U.S., but are unable to persist through the duplicative, overly complicated, and costly permitting process. Commercial companies have reported spending nearly five years and $1 million preparing for and completing applications without ever being granted a permit and have ultimately developed in other nations.
"The offshore aquaculture industry will continue to grow around the world in the coming decades - the only question is, will the U.S. have a role in this burgeoning, sustainable seafood industry? The U.S. and particularly Florida are uniquely positioned to provide a streamlined and environmentally-sustainable regulatory climate for offshore aquaculture, but not until federal permitting barriers are addressed.
"In my capacity as Florida's 12th Commissioner of Agriculture, where our Department assists in the development of aquaculture and regulates aquafarms through our Division of Aquaculture, I bring these issues before you today. As Florida's aquaculture industry continues to grow, so does the need for streamlined federal policies for offshore aquaculture to thrive. Today's hearing is an important step, helping shine a light on this developing industry and the critical role federal policies play in either hindering or encouraging its continued growth.
"I hope that this Committee, the Congress, and our federal agencies will work together to enact common-sense policies to advance sustainable offshore aquaculture to the benefit of American farmers, the jobs they support, and the families they feed. As Florida's Commissioner of Agriculture, our Department and Division of Aquaculture stand ready to assist in this effort."
MSTRUCK-6894255 TeamManager

Senate Appropriations Committee Issues Report on 2020 Commerce, Justice, Science Appropriations Bill (Part 3 of 10)

Publication info: Targeted News Service ; Washington, D.C. [Washington, D.C]01 Oct 2019.
